        <p class="date">
         Fri Oct 04, 2013 at 05:41 AM PDT
        </p>
        <h2>
         <a href="/story/2013/10/04/1243700/-The-Oceans-are-Dying-Oxygen-is-Depleting-Acidity-Rising-at-Fastest-Rate-in-300-000-000-Years" id="titleHref">
          The Oceans are Dying: Oxygen is Depleting, Acidity Rising at Fastest Rate in 300,000,000 Years
         </a>
         <span style="white-space: nowrap">
         </span>
        </h2>
        <p class="author">
         <span>
          by
         </span>
         <a href="/user/FishOutofWater">
          FishOutofWater
         </a>
         <a alt="Follow this author" class="ajax type-user do-hotlist do-updateContent size-tiny off altTooltip" href="#?friend_id=94755&amp;is_stream=1">
          Follow
         </a>
         <span class="for">
          for
         </span>
         <a href="/blog/Ocean Advocates">
          Ocean Advocates
         </a>
        </p>
        <ul class="front-page-share clearfix">
         <li style="margin-left: 0px">
          <div class="fb-share-button" data-href="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2013/10/04/1243700/-The-Oceans-are-Dying-Oxygen-is-Depleting-Acidity-Rising-at-Fastest-Rate-in-300-000-000-Years" data-layout="button_count">
          </div>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="twitter-share-button" data-count="horizontal" data-related="markos" data-text="The Oceans are Dying: Oxygen is Depleting, Acidity Rising at Fastest Rate in 300,000,000 Years" data-url="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2013/10/04/1243700/-The-Oceans-are-Dying-Oxygen-is-Depleting-Acidity-Rising-at-Fastest-Rate-in-300-000-000-Years" data-via="dailykos" href="https://twitter.com/share">
           Tweet
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="comments comment_count_with_new" data-comment_count="215" data-story_id="1243700" href="/story/2013/10/04/1243700/-The-Oceans-are-Dying-Oxygen-is-Depleting-Acidity-Rising-at-Fastest-Rate-in-300-000-000-Years#comments">
           215 Comments
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </div>
       <div class="article-body">
        <div id="intro">
         <div class="dkimg-c">
          <span class="image_container">
           <img alt="Hypoxic Zone" height="400" src="http://s3.amazonaws.com/dk-production/images/51519/large/GulfDeadZoneImageNOAA.jpg?1380813796" width="500"/>
          </span>
         </div>
         Oxygen levels are dropping and ocean waters are acidifying at the fastest rate in at least 300 million years when the greatest marine extinction in earth's history took place according to
         <a href="http://www.stateoftheocean.org/pdfs/IPSO-PR-2013-FINAL.pdf">
          The State of the Ocean Report 2013
         </a>
         written by an international panel of marine scientists.
         <p>
          Today's explosive increase in human CO2 emissions and warming of the oceans are recreating the conditions of the great Permian extinction 300 million years ago when massive volcanic eruptions in Siberia triggered the release of enormous amounts of stored carbon. A leading theory is that
          <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event">
           deoxygenation and acidification of the oceans led to the bacterial production of toxic hydrogen sulfide gas which poisoned species dependent on oxygen.
          </a>
          By the end of this natural catastrophe 90% to 95% of all marine species were extinct. The biodiversity of the oceans took 30 million years for to recover.
         </p>
         <p>
          <big>
           The next mass extinction event may have already begun.
          </big>
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          the scale and rate of the present day carbon perturbation, and resulting ocean acidification, is unprecedented in Earths known history. Todays rate of carbon release, at approximately 30Gt of CO2 per year, is at least 10 times faster than that which preceded the last major species extinction (the Paleocene Eocene Thermal Maximum extinction, or PETM, ca. 55 million years ago), while geological records indicate that the current acidification is unparalleled in at least the last 300 million years. We are entering an unknown territory of marine ecosystem change, and exposing organisms to intolerable evolutionary pressure. The next mass extinction event may have already begun.
         </blockquote>
         <big>
          A "deadly trio of acidification, warming and deoxygenation
         </big>
         <br/>
         <center>
          ~
         </center>
         <div class="dkimg-l">
          <span class="image_container">
           <img alt="" height="225" src="http://s3.amazonaws.com/dk-production/images/51599/small/OceanicOxygenProfile.jpg?1380836629" width="200"/>
          </span>
         </div>
         Human CO2 emissions directly cause both global warming and ocean acidification. But that's just the beginning. Mixing tends to decline in warming waters because a warm fresh surface layer is substantially lighter than colder middle and deep water.
         <a href="http://earthguide.ucsd.edu/virtualmuseum/climatechange1/06_1.shtml">
          The surface layer tends to float and not mix.
         </a>
         Organic carbon is always falling from the surface to deeper waters. Bacteria oxidize the fallen carbon to CO2. This process reduces oxygen levels and increases the acidity of the water. When the rate of mixing declines the residence time of water in a layer increases, so acidity levels tend to rise and oxygen levels drop in layers below the surface as the climate warms.
         <p>
          <a href="http://theworldlink.com/news/local/key-species-mysteriously-dying-off-oregon-coast/article_6e2fdea8-df49-11e2-9f23-0019bb2963f4.html">
           Hypoxic - low oxygen - water may be already killing keystone species in the Pacific northwest.
          </a>
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          COOS BAY  Something is killing large numbers of a keystone species off the Oregon Coast. Federal researchers say it could spell danger for the regions other marine life.
          <p>
           Bill Peterson, an oceanographer with the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administrations Hatfield Marine Science Center in Newport, said in the past few weeks millions of dead North Pacific krill have washed up on beaches between Newport and Eureka, Calif.
          </p>
          <p>
           Peterson said its the largest die-off hes aware of in recent history. ...
          </p>
          <p>
           Joe Tyburczy, a researcher with the California Sea Grant extension office who has been looking into the dead krill with Peterson, said oceanographic cruises along the northern California coast did find lower oxygen levels than usually seen in Pacific Northwest waters.
          </p>
          <p>
           If it is hypoxia, theres a possibility of implications for other species like crab, he said.
          </p>
         </blockquote>
         Acidification of sea water in Washington State oyster hatcheries killed the developing oysters.
         <a href="http://bcove.me/6ntxr4qy">
          Please watch this outstanding video on ocean acidification.
         </a>
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          <a href="http://apps.seattletimes.com/reports/sea-change/2013/sep/11/oysters-hit-hard/">
           Oysters started dying by the billions along the Northwest coast in 2005
          </a>
          , and have been struggling ever since. When scientists cautiously linked the deaths to plummeting ocean pH in 2008 and 2009, few outside the West Coasts $110 million industry believed it.
          <p>
           By the time scientists confirmed it early last year, the regions several hundred oyster growers had become a global harbinger  the first tangible sign anywhere in the world that ocean acidification already was walloping marine life and hurting people.
          </p>
         </blockquote>
         Richard Feely and a team of scientists from Pacific Marine Environmental Lab were stunned to discover cold, acidic, low-oxygen water welling up to the surface along the northern California coast in 2007. Scientists had not expected acidification to hit the west coast for 50 to 100 years. Dr. Feely published his work and word of it reached oyster farmers who's hatcheries were failing.
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          The oyster farmers invited Feely to their annual conference.
          <p>
           Feely explained that when north winds blew, deep ocean water was drawn right to the beach, which meant this newly corrosive water probably got sucked into the hatchery. That same water also flowed into the Strait of Juan de Fuca and made its way to Hood Canal.
          </p>
          <p>
           The oyster industry pleaded with Congress, which supplied money for new equipment. Over several years, the hatcheries tested their water using high-tech pH sensors. When the pH was low, it was very low and baby oysters died within two days. By drawing water only when the pH was normal, shellfish production got back on track.
          </p>
          <p>
           They told us it was like turning on headlights on a car  it was so clear what was going on, Feely said.
          </p>
         </blockquote>
         Moreover, because processes in the ocean are slow to change this deadly water would continue to affect the Pacific northwest for another 50 years if all human CO2 emissions stopped today. It will take 30 to 50 years for the most acidic water already present along the west coast to well up. This is the beginning of a disaster that we cannot stop. The best we can do is to keep it from growing far larger and far more deadly. The changes happening in the waters of the Pacific northwest are the first stages of a global marine catastrophe if CO2 emissions are not rapidly reduced.
         <p>
          <big>
           Deadly trio will have cascading consequences for marine biology &amp; humans
          </big>
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          It is the simultaneous occurrence of the deadly trio of acidification, warming and deoxygenation that is seriously effecting how productive and efficient the ocean is, as temperatures, chemistry, surface stratification, nutrient and oxygen supply are all implicated, meaning that many organisms will find themselves in unsuitable environments.
          <p>
           <a href="http://www.stateoftheocean.org/pdfs/IPSO-Summary-Oct13-FINAL.pdf">
            These impacts will have cascading consequences for marine biology, including altered food web dynamics and the expansion of pathogens.
           </a>
           To make matters even worse, this is all happening to marine ecosystems already undermined by other human pressures such as overfishing, eutrophication and pollution.
          </p>
          <p>
           The adaption of species to these altered conditions is in some cases possible  as is migration, though as warming demands a poleward migration while acidification encourages the movement to warmer more equatorial waters the green pastures will become increasingly scarce and competition for them fierce. Mass extinctions happen in the geological equivalent of overnight; we may already have entered into an extinction period and not yet realized it. What is certain is that current carbon perturbations will have huge implications for humans, and may well be the most important challenge faced since the first hominids evolved.
          </p>
         </blockquote>
        </div>
        <p class="divider-doodle">
        </p>

        <div>
         <p>
          Add keywords that describe this diary.
		Separate multiple keywords with commas.
          <br/>
          <a href="/special/tag_tips" target="_blank">
           Tagging tips
          </a>
          -
          <a href="/search?search_tags=1" target="_blank">
           Search For Tags
          </a>
          -
          <a href="http://www.dkosopedia.com/wiki/Tag:Tagfinder" target="_blank">
           Browse For Tags
          </a>

         <div class="pageTip hasTooltip" style="margin-top:3px">

         </div>
         <div class="tooltipContents qtip-style-dk4_large qtip-pos-br">
          <p>
           <b>
            More Tagging tips:
           </b>
          </p>
          <p>
           A tag is a way to search for this diary. If someone is searching for "Barack Obama," is this a diary they'd be trying to find?
          </p>
          <p>
           Use a person's full name, without any title. Senator Obama may become President Obama, and Michelle Obama might run for office.
          </p>
          <p>
           If your diary covers an election or elected official, use election tags, which are generally the state abbreviation followed by the office. 
		CA-01 is the first district House seat. CA-Sen covers both senate races. NY-GOV covers the New York governor's race.
          </p>
          <p>
           Tags do not compound: that is, "education reform" is a completely different tag from "education". A tag like "reform" alone is probably not meaningful.
          </p>
         </div>
         <!-- &lt;h4 id="p_tagErrorMessage"&gt;You must enter at least one Tag for this Diary Entry:&lt;/h4&gt; -->
         <p>
          <input id="p_tagInput" name="tag_draft" size="70" type="text" value=""/>
          <!-- &lt;input id="tagSubmit" type="submit" name="submit" value="Submit" /&gt; -->
         </p>
         <p style="font-style:italic">
          Consider if one or more of these tags fits your diary:
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Civil Rights');return false;">
           Civil Rights
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Community');return false;">
           Community
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Congress');return false;">
           Congress
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Culture');return false;">
           Culture
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Economy');return false;">
           Economy
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Education');return false;">
           Education
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Elections');return false;">
           Elections
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Energy');return false;">
           Energy
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Environment');return false;">
           Environment
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Health Care');return false;">
           Health Care
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('International');return false;">
           International
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Labor');return false;">
           Labor
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Law');return false;">
           Law
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Media');return false;">
           Media
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Meta');return false;">
           Meta
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('National Security');return false;">
           National Security
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Science');return false;">
           Science
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Transportation');return false;">
           Transportation
          </a>
          , or
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('White House');return false;">
           White House
          </a>
          .
	If your diary is specific to a state, consider adding the state 
	(
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('California');return false;">
           California
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Texas');return false;">
           Texas
          </a>
          , etc).
	Keep in mind, though, that there are many wonderful and important diaries
	that don't fit in any of these tags. Don't worry if yours doesn't.
         </p>

         <li id="c201">
          <div class="cx" id="cx51526649">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             The largest animal on the planet...
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1243700/51526649#c201?mode=alone;showrate=1#c201">
              0+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             the blue whale, lives by feeding on arguably the smallest animal on the planet, plankton.
            </p>
            <p>
             The smartest animal on the planet, arguably, is probably dependant on that same plankton, for survival.
            </p>
            <p>
             If the oceans die, we probably die.
            </p>
           </div>

            has already begun, see e.g. "The Sixth Extinction." The big question is one of how far it will go. Today's geologic era is what Jason W. Moore properly calls the "capitalocene" -- the era in which capital accumulation reshapes the natural world.

                 ", which bills itself as providing "Humanist Counter-Theory in the Age of Misandry".
                </p>
                <p>
                 That's right. The Real Problem per anime1973 is that men are oppressed by a "misandrist" society. And any post suggesting otherwise (or the opposite) is HR-worthy, because of, um,
                 <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/comments/1243700/51519045#c173">
                  bigotry
                 </a>
                 . Kind of like how calling out and naming white supremacy is, y'know, bigotry against white people.
                </p>
                <p>
                 Not sure what the consequences are for serial HR abuse, but I hope to find out.
                </p>
                <p>
                 <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2013/08/23/1233352/-New-community-guidelines-final-drafts">
                  Site rules
                 </a>
                 :
                 <br/>
                </p>
                <blockquote>
                 Our new reporting tools make it easier to track hidden comments and who HR'd them. If we determine that a comment should not have been hidden, those dropping the Hide Ratings will lose their ratings ability for a period of time, progressively longer for each infraction until that ability is removed forever. Uprating personal insults is as bad if not worse than making the insult itself because this rewards the insulters and encourages them to continue the same behavior. Doing so will likewise cost users their ratings privileges for a period, with long penalties for repeat infractions. In baseball, a tie goes to the runner. At Daily Kos, any gray area will be decided in favor of the commenter. So if you're not sure that something should be HR'd, then don't. Because if the situation is that iffy, chances are that it'll be you who gets burned. HRs are for clear and obvious violations.
                </blockquote>
                I think if anime would like to stick around and keep ratings privileges, s/he should be careful.

           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             get away with it for generations. It doesn't work that way in the natural world. When you steal from nature, you are robbing your children and future generations.
            </p>
            <p>
             Mother nature coldly extinguishes species that expand out of control. They are called "index fossils" by geologists. Index fossils are used to identify specific times in the sedimentary record.
            </p>

                    </dt>
                    <dd>

                     <a href="/user/uid:229504">

                     </a>
                     ,
                     <a href="/user/uid:81763">
                      Eikyu Saha
                     </a>
                    </dd>
                   </dl>
                  </div>
                  <div class="ct">
                   <p>
                    I'm not willing to call mass extinctions "fine." Consequences lasting millions of years matter. Carbon pollution needs to be halted, and we need to find a way to do it. Objecting that it 'probably won't happen' is unhelpful.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    When something truly is imperative, as this is, then one finds a way to make it happen.
                    <br/>

               <div class="ct">
                <p>
                 as a non-yank.
                </p>
                <p>
                 America is the outcome of a way of thinking unleashed on a very rich continent with very little in the way of resistance to its methods and processes.
                </p>
                <p>
                 The way of thinking that resulted in "America" would have died very rapidly in, say, the Sahara.
                </p>
                <p>
                 And when the resources that produced America run out, cheap, plentiful energy, water and fertility, America will go away and the people who lived America will either become something that is not "America" or they will become extinct because they are adapted to perfectly to the America model.
                </p>
                <p>
                 To blame individual, or even collective Americans as greedy, stupid etc is to see it upside down. Americans are simply fully adapted residents of Americanness
                </p>
               </div>

                 <span class="cu">
                  I agree with non-yank DD
                 </span>
                 <span class="crd ntb">
                  (
                  <a href="/comments/1243700/51519414#c177?mode=alone;showrate=1#c177">
                   2+ / 0-
                  </a>

                <div class="crs">
                 <dl>
                  <dt>
                   Recommended by:
                  </dt>
                  <dd>
                   <a href="/user/uid:235212">
                    Joieau
                   </a>
                   ,
                   <a href="/user/uid:171">
                    Deep Dark
                   </a>
                  </dd>
                 </dl>
                </div>
                <div class="ct">
                 <p>
                  We give too much credit to individual choice. It makes us feel safe to think we are in control of our destiny. It also makes us feel superior to call our fortune a matter of "choice" while watching others fail. But for us to resist the entirety of our historical trajectory means fighting all battles on all fronts at the same time. Our computers use precious metals mined most often under inhuman conditions; everything we consume has a toxic effect to Earth, to the biosphere, to the social sphere. Even the poor miners are complicit in this, whether they know it or not, until they die at an early age. We Yanks, over here in cushy-cushy land, can slow the tide a tiny bit, perhaps, and delay the inevitable just long enough for someone new to come along and delay it a few minutes more, but bigger tides pull on us.
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  DD has it right: we are the products of our environment far more than the other way around.
                 </p>

              "Reagan's dead, and he was a lousy president" -- Keith Olbermann 4/22/09
             </p>
             <p class="cb">
              by
              <a href="/user/uid:65471">
               kovie
              </a>

          <li id="c158">
           <div class="cx" id="cx51517285">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              Lot of people have been working on that one for
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1243700/51517285#c158?mode=alone;showrate=1#c158">
               1+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:330564">
                JosephK74
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              decades, mostly through the UN. Just over 40 countries have stable or declining populations. A larger group are moving toward stability but aren't there yet (including CHina and the US). Then there are some countries who still aren't on track to stabilize.
             </p>
             <p>
              The good news is that with good policies (including education for girls and wide access to inexpensive birth control,) population growth rates can change pretty fast. Iran, under Khomeini in the early 1980's, had a growth rate approaching the biological maximum. After his death, policies changed dramatically, and by 1993, Iran had one of the lowest growth rates in the developing world.
             </p>
             <p>
              I used to read predictions that the world population would peak between 8 and 10 billion (depending on how well we did with control efforts), somewhere between 2040 and 2100, and then begin to decline. People talked about whether it would be possible to avoid widespread famine, and if so, how.
             </p>
             <p>
              Global warming changes that calculus. THe world is consciously moving toward containing population growth, but I don't think there's much chance now that it'll happen without starvation playing a grim role.
             </p>
             <p>
              Population policy remains enormously important. Famine and disease have never been enough. Europe lost a third of its population in the Black Plague, recouped that astonishingly fast, and kept growing. Only conscious, intelligent policy works.
             </p>
            </div>

              </p>
              <p>
               For example, many sources say pH is a scale from 0 - 14. It is not. pH is the negative logarithm of the activity of H+ ion (which doesn't actually exist) in aqueous solution. Theoretically pH could be negative or greater than 14, but measuring pH in extremely acidic or basic solutions is problematic.
              </p>
              <p>
               pH is not the concentration of H+ in solution for solutions of high ionic strength. In reality, water molecules tend to cluster. In high ionic strength solutions the ions break up the water clusters, effectively increasing the H+ activity in those solutions.
              </p>
              <p>
               The more you know about pH the more complicated it gets. I have just scratched the surface in this comment.
              </p>
              <p>
               Yes, Wikipedia is our friend.
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="cf">
              <p class="sig">
               look for my eSci diary series Thursday evening.

          <li id="c43">
           <div class="cx" id="cx51511137">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              Per Capita Means Nothing
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1243700/51511137#c43?mode=alone;showrate=1#c43">
               2+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:35014">
                NYFM
               </a>
               ,
               <a href="/user/uid:152668">
                IreGyre
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              What does it mean that China and India have hundreds of millions of people who produce little carbon pollution? They have hundreds of millions more who produce more pollution than fewer people in the USA. The per capita pollution is a way for countries with really dirty industries to hide behind large populations who they exclude from economic or political opportunity, while joined with other industrialized countries to pollute.
             </p>
             <p>
              The US produces about a quarter of the world's stuff. That means the rest of the world outsources the production of what it consumes to the US. Where our production is slightly better than average pollution per value produced for everyone. Which is why it's imperative the US reduce its pollution, and drive the other polluters to reduce too. But not because of any specious per capita measurement.
             </p>
            </div>

            <li id="c66">
             <div class="cx" id="cx51511647">
              <h5>
               <a class="de">
               </a>
               <span class="cu">
                Equal Value
               </span>
               <span class="crd ntb">
                (
                <a href="/comments/1243700/51511647#c66?mode=alone;showrate=1#c66">
                 1+ / 0-
                </a>
                )
               </span>
              </h5>
              <div class="crs">
               <dl>
                <dt>
                 Recommended by:
                </dt>
                <dd>
                 <a href="/user/uid:152668">
                  IreGyre
                 </a>
                </dd>
               </dl>
              </div>
              <div class="ct">
               <p>
                The human lives are of course of equal value. But the pollution system doesn't run on any kind of per capita quota.
               </p>
               <p>
                The US produces 25% of the world's stuff. Yes, we also import about 25% of the world's stuff. But the 25% we produce is produced more efficiently, with less pollution per value, than the rest of the world's stuff that we import. If their stuff we import were produced at the US level of pollution, we'd be a large part of the way to reducing the pollution imbalance that is warming the planet. But we're not.
               </p>
               <p>
                That extra pollution from the rest of the world's dirtier production isn't a consequence of per capita anything. Per capita is a spurious detail. What matters is the efficiency of production, and the US is better than the rest of the world. Especially it's far better than production in China and India, and practically everyplace where per capita pollution is lower. Because those people, while equally valuable and even more exposed to Greenhouse catastrophes, are not involved in the polluting system. While nearly every American is involved - which is keeping the pollution down.
               </p>
              </div>

            <div class="cf">
             <p class="sig">
              We are all pupils in the eyes of God.
             </p>
             <p class="cb">
              by
              <a href="/user/uid:312579">
               nuclear winter solstice
              </a>
              on
              <a href="/comments/1243700/51511108#c41">
               Fri Oct 04, 2013 at 07:12:16 AM PDT
              </a>
             </p>
             <p class="cl">

              <a href="#c32">

          </li>
          <ul class="cm i1" id="k51511108">
           <li id="c168">
            <div class="cx" id="cx51517900">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               Oh, most life forms metabolize
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/1243700/51517900#c168?mode=alone;showrate=1#c168">
                1+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:312579">
                 nuclear winter solstice
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               radioactive isotopes just fine. Including plankton, and humans (and just about everything in between). Strontium is used as calcium - builds strong teeth and bones! Cesium is used like potassium - vital in a number of biochemical activities and retained in muscle tissue. Like hearts. The heavy metals are bone-seekers too, but will also do great damage to organs. Like livers, kidneys, etc.
              </p>
              <p>
               Quite a lot that doesn't spontaneously disintegrate on its way through - thereby zapping sensitive internal cells like the single cell thick lining to stomach and intestines point-blank - goes out with the garbage, but some of it is retained and incorporated into tissues. You are what you eat, after all.
              </p>
              <p>
               Problem with unstable isotopes (atoms of a given element) is that they're unstable. When incorporated into biological molecules, their self-destruction will also destroy the molecules they're incorporated into. And anything near that, too.
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="cf">
              <p class="sig">
              </p>
              <p class="cb">
               by
               <a href="/user/uid:235212">

Huge temperature differential generators in the ocean could in theory introduce more mixing and be a source of non polluting energy BUT the placement of these things and how many would be needed balanced with harm avoidance... done wrong it could still devastate some ocean ecologies short term at least.

         <li id="c116">
          <div class="cx" id="cx51514261">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             The amount of oxygen dissolved in water
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1243700/51514261#c116?mode=alone;showrate=1#c116">
              1+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:94755">
               FishOutofWater
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             depends on temperature. The higher the temperature, the lower the maximum amount of oxygen that can exist in solution.
            </p>
            <p>
             The presence of biological oxygen demand (BOD) will further reduce oxygen concentrations to the extent of the the concentration of BOD in ocean water [higher BOD will occur from polluted agricultural runoff or discharge of poorly treated municipal wastewater.
             <br/>
            </p>
           </div>

            <a href="http://articles.philly.com/2013-09-23/news/42294649_1_climate-change-ken-able-species">

            </a>
            <br/>
           </p>
           <blockquote>
            "As far as fishermen are concerned, climate change is here. This is a reality," said Tom Fote, of the Jersey Coast Anglers Association. "We're going to have to change the way we fish."
            <p>

            </p>
            <p>
             Within the last two decades, New Jersey's renowned surf clams, once common just off the beaches and harvested for chowder and fried clams, have moved 40 miles offshore into deeper, cooler water.
            </p>

             Yeah, we were so good at the geoengineering we've done so far that we're facing extinction. Right.

Which probably means giant fusion plants at sea, or some other tech leap that remains out of even science's grasp, let alone engineering's, industry's, economics' or politics'.

        <li id="c40">
         <div class="cx" id="cx51511092">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            It's Done
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1243700/51511092#c40?mode=alone;showrate=1#c40">
             10+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:851082">
              Darwinian Detrius
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:145342">
              Carol in San Antonio
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:120208">
              TomFromNJ
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:217155">
              No one gets out alive
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:219522">
              BlueDragon
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:65319">
              Paul Ferguson
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:96369">
              Matt Z
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:909137">
              sillycarrot
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:161824">
              marsanges
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:72100">
              greenearth
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            The IPCC report indicated that if we put a trillion extra tons of carbon into the atmosphere beyond pre-industrial (~1700) levels, we'd certainly raise temperatures at least +2C above pre-industrial levels. We have already put about 750 billion tons. And of course those are conservative (in every sense of the word) estimates.
           </p>
           <p>
            But now with fracking natgas, other shale gas and oil, coal so cheap it hovers above going out of business so gets massive subsidies... we're going to pump that last 250 billion tons into the atmosphere. Every day many more people get the means to consume more energy and dump more pollution, both as a percentage of the population and as a larger population. The pollution rate is hurtling upwards, not slowing or reversing. We are going to race past +2C, probably +5C. The difference between today and the last Ice Age was only about +8C, so we're going to see those kinds of changes, but in the more energetic direction.
           </p>
           <p>
            We should do as much as we can to conserve energy, to reduce pollution, to increase efficiency. Slowing the inevitable is still better than embracing it, because it means the destruction of civilization (probably when climate stress pushes WMD war which engulfs us all). But we should be focusing on alternative energy that can power whatever can aggressively remove carbon from the atmosphere and oceans. Huge fusion plants at sea that suck CO2 from the ocean winds into nonbiodegradable skeletons for new reefs, that kind of really big new industry. That is the only race now left that can be won. Except maybe building arks to survive the destruction.
           </p>
           <p>
            The main change we must accept is that our economics and politics proved a suicide pact. If we survive we must somehow encode in our culture the wisdom never to repeat it. Or we will, and any recovery over the centuries will be inevitably followed by another apocalypse we make for ourselves.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            "When the going gets weird, the weird
            <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/user/DocGonzo/diary" rel="nofollow">
             turn pro
            </a>
            ." - HST
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:51671">
             DocGonzo
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1243700/51511092#c40">
             Fri Oct 04, 2013 at 07:10:54 AM PDT
            </a>
           </p>

          <div class="cx" id="cx51517541">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             We have to get the wisdom before we can encode it.
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1243700/51517541#c162?mode=alone;showrate=1#c162">
              0+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             Early on, carbon pollution was innocent: we didn't know what it was doing. But later... How is it that greed can become addictive, compulsive, driving people beyond any actual increase of pleasure or meaning or joy, driving people to blind destruction? And what is it about corporate structures that feeds that addiction and blind compulsiveness? Our basic nature as human beings isn't going to change. We have to figure out what it is in our corporate economic system that has caused smart people in a free country to act so irrationally and in such astonishingly poisonous ways. Because if we don't figure it out, we can't regulate or avoid it, and (assuming we survive) it'll happen again.
            </p>
           </div>

          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Does anyone see a problem with this argument? Does anyone here at Daily Kos understand that this is not a functional argument. We must reduce our generation of CO2. The cost argument is essentially that we refuse to accept any burden to save the future. Every time I read this argument I see red.
           </p>
           <p>

           </p>
           <p>

          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            A lot of hopelessness in this thread. I feel you.
           </p>
           <p>
            When I get down I go over the
            <a href="http://cleantechnica.com/">
             CleanTechnica
            </a>
            and read about the rapid pace of technological change and adoption of renewable energy. And the pace is increasing. Economic factors are driving it, not just policy.
           </p>
           <p>
            But it's up to us to fight for policies that won't hinder the growth of renewables. Utilities are fighting back against feed-in tariffs and other measures to increase the rate of adoption for rooftop solar. We're at the "then they fight you" stage of this thing. The next stage is "then you win."
           </p>
           <p>
            Imagine if we were able to significantly cut into the profits of fossil fuel companies. Imagine if they could no longer afford to just buy all of our politicians. Put them out of business and we win the whole match. This isn't just about the environment, it's about
            <strong>
             <em>
              every single fight we want the Democrats to win
             </em>
            </strong>
            .
           </p>

           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             and more and better ones are developing to the point that they will just win due to direct economic factors... cheaper etc.
            </p>
            <p>
             BUT the overlap of waning dirty energy... rising delayed climate changes still playing out... and the replacement of old energy with renewable green energy could still leave the world struggling to endure a period of destabilization of just about everything... natural, social and economic.
            </p>
            <p>
             We do not know the full extent of the still developing harm... changing technological course of a whole planet is exactly like the Titanic and the iceberg... takes time to slow, steer or reverse an ocean liner.
            </p>
            <p>
             Or are we like the Costa Concordia speeding mostly heedlessly past an ignored danger and taking a while to finally sink... most survive but a catastrophe... and the long term situation dealt with via a huge and unprecedented use of technology rather than let it work itself out "naturally".
            </p>

           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1243700/51512650#c87?mode=alone;showrate=1#c87">
             5+ / 0-

          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            and sometimes you eat the bear.
           </p>
           <p>
            i'd say nobody knows how it's gonna play out. the planet, like the human body or any animal, is a lot more variable and surprising. we may implode next year. or we may find that installing solar panels on every rooftop is a good way to offset all that heat and energy... or we cobble together all the ocean garbage and create makeshift glaciers (cover them with white growing organisms??? i don't know... just spitballing here) ... but most important: simply stop playing the game of western capitalism and move off that board and create new rules and systems, and we might get another shot at that bear.
           </p>
           <p>
            i don't know how many times it needs to be said or the correct way to say it: participating in American democracy at the national level is A FUCKING WASTE OF TIME. they ain't gonna change the game. they don't know how. they don't believe it, and they can't grasp the scale of it... they, the few the 1%, are the real deniers of the destruction their policies bring to all of us. we need to get back to our communities and do some trickle up politics.
           </p>
           <p>
            consider the sig line:
           </p>

            </span>

           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             sometimes, but NEVER a BS'er and like most true visionaries, always worth remembering.
            </p>
            <p>
             We do really need new models, and there are many people who have been working on good ones for decades. The trick now is to start getting them off the shelf and into action, so people have a chance to see them in action.
            </p>
            <p>
             Local action, on an ostensibly purely practical level like the community garden/ beekeeping/ etc. movement, can have profound political effects once they have some momentum and draw other locals in by being just plain fun!
            </p>
            <p>
             Empowerment is catching, but unlike measles, there is no vaccine for it. (maybe that should be my sigline!)
            </p>

         <li id="c187">
          <div class="cx" id="cx51520344">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             We know, we have known for a while. BF knew
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1243700/51520344#c187?mode=alone;showrate=1#c187">
              1+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:87771">
               pfiore8
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             Most of us just don't want to admit that we know how its going to play out, that we have done this to our children and grandchildren, that we are the ones who could have made a difference and didn't.
            </p>
            <p>
             Lets take your Buckminster Fuller quote as an example. We have lots of new models that make even the current IPCC modeling obsolete. It is a problem that there are seven billion of us not helping change the focus from everything else to climate change mediation.
            </p>
            <p>
             To build what consensus can be built the least common denominator for any acceptable model has to be set so incredibly low that it really isn't usable, and any model set higher gets painted as hysterical because it exceeds the calm rational scientific IPCC consensus.
            </p>
            <p>
             If you want to model any observable organic oceanic process from Lion Fish invading the reefs of the Caribbean and Atlantic to the die offs of shellfish on the Northwest coast start with the Fibonacci series and allow that the rate of increase is going to increase at an existing rate until it breaks down, thats how its going to play out.
            </p>
           </div>

             0+ / 0-

          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Firstly, thanks for the diary and all the work you do to help keep the community informed.
           </p>
           <p>
            Now back to my question. I realize nobody knows the answer to this and there are too many inter-related variables to even make a realistically informed guess. But you must think about it? And given the vast amount of information you monitor and study, somewhere deep inside you must have an opinion.
           </p>
           <p>
            If nothing changes in terms of human output in the next decade, then how much time do we have?
           </p>

            I don't really expect an answer because it is a terrifying question to contemplate, but I would like to know what you think.
           </p>

          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Just terrible to read and think about.  We need to
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1243700/51516195#c127?mode=alone;showrate=1#c127">
             2+ / 0-
            </a>

          <div class="crs">

          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            hop to deal with this scary scientific report.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            Point Acorn
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:192262">
             Boston Beans
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1243700/51516195#c127">

        <li id="c157">
         <div class="cx" id="cx51517260">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Maybe not in 65 m.y. rather than 300
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1243700/51517260#c157?mode=alone;showrate=1#c157">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            There was another extremely catastrophic, nearly instantaneous, mass extinction at the end of the Cretaceous that was certainly faster and likely more devastating than the one we are living through. This was only(?) 65 million years ago. The K/Pg boundary in most relatively shallow marine sites records an anoxic event (now preserved as pyrite in the sediment). The asteroid impact that caused this may well have heated the atmosphere in hours and acidified the upper ocean in days. Global atmospheric heating (enough to set plants afire and burn unprotected animals) is caused by re-entry of ejecta into the upper atmosphere at several km/sec. Acidification might have two sources: ionization of atmospheric nitrogen gas to produce acid rain or more prolonged rain of sulfuric acid produced from sulfates from the Yucatan crater. Nitric acid rain is disregarded by many as equilibrium models suggest not enough will be produced, but some workers think kinetic processes could produce lots of NOx molecules. After all this insult the Earth probably experienced months or years of cold and dark due to lower transparency of the stratosphere caused by SO2. The balance of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere is not understood at all.
           </p>
           <p>
            It's not really saying much that our mass extinction will only be the second worst in the last 300 m.y., but the Earth has had some very bad days in its history. Life will go on, but as the dinosaurs learned not all species will.
           </p>
          </div>

           <p class="sig">

        <li id="c189">
         <div class="cx" id="cx51520353">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Don't know about you but I'm OK
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1243700/51520353#c189?mode=alone;showrate=1#c189">
             1+ / 0-

          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:209994">
              too many people
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            I drive a hybrid Honda, shop at Wild Oats, drink nothing but micro-brew and Fiji Water, and Amazon calls once a week with the latest in organic cotton fashion. I just heard that US energy worries are over thanks to huge new reserves of carbon based natural gas, and I am feeling most smug and secure at the top of the global distribution system.......................
           </p>
           <p>
            Seriously folks we are all, as hard as it is to admit try as we might to be good, living a the top of the food chain as the human population approaches 8 billion, 2/3 of us at reproductive age or younger. Demographers project continued growth to 12 billion or a crash starting around mid-century to 6. Take your pick; either way life in the near future is going to be nasty. Collapse is coming quietly now, but the lamentations of starving billions with a tympani of exploded nuclear bombs will be the song of the future.
           </p>
           <p>
            Recommended reading:
           </p>
           <p>
            UNDER A GREEN SKY: Global Warming the Mass Extinctions of the Past and What They Can Tell Us About Our Future
           </p>

            Peter Ward
           </p>

          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            No Krill, no baleen whales.
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1243700/51520578#c192?mode=alone;showrate=1#c192">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Hypoxia won't affect other whale species directly, but it will affect the food they eat. Dolphins, Orcas, and toothed whales will see their food supply die.
           </p>
           <p>
            This is a disaster of proportions that the human race has never seen.
           </p>
           <p>
            We're at a stage now where unless we find a way to create microenvironments in captivity to shepherd these species until the oceans repair themselves, we're not going to have any of these species left at all.
           </p>
           <p>
            This is something that can kill just about everything in the ocean. Almost everything.
           </p>

        <li id="c202">
         <div class="cx" id="cx51526711">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            What is most noteworthy is that this is news.
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1243700/51526711#c202?mode=alone;showrate=1#c202">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Environmentalists have warned of this trend for decades now. The coral reefs are dying back at an alarming rate. We seem to have reached a tipping point where these factors catalyze one another, bringing about a "perfect storm" of destruction of marine species.
           </p>
           <p>
            We have recklessly poisoned the waterways, the atmosphere, and the earth. I live in the shadow of an active volcano that constantly emits toxic gases. As ocean levels rise, we should expect more seismic and more volcanic activity, with the release of more carbon to acidify the oceans and increase warming. We are in an environmental free fall from which many species, possibly including humans, will not survive. We are already seeing extinctions at an alarming rate. Our arrogance in thinking that we can control the environment is about to bite us in the collective ass.
           </p>
          </div>

  <script type="text/javascript">
   if($.cookie('meter_user') !== 'false') {
		if ($('#ePostButton').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('#ePostButton').click(function() { 
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Posted Comment', { 
		      repliedTo: $(this).parents('#eP').prevAll('.cb').children('a').first().html() 
		    });
		  });
		}
		
		if (!!document.location.pathname.match('activate') &amp;&amp; $('h1:contains("Welcome to Daily Kos!")').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Signed Up');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname === '/') {
		  if (document.location.hostname.match(/^www|^dailykos/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Front Page');
		  } else {
		    var subdomain = document.location.hostname.split('.')[0];
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Vertical', { name: subdomain });
		  }
		}
		
		if ($('.ratinate').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.ratinate').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Comment') });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-addRating.type-story').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-addRating.type-story').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Story') });
		}
		
		if ($("form[action$='publishHow']").length &gt; 0) {
		  $(document).ready(function() {
			  $('.type-story.do-publishHow').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Published Story') });
			 });
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/user/) &amp;&amp; $('li.here').length &gt; 0) {
		  if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Stream/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Stream', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Profile/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Profile', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:last li.here').children('a').html().match(/My Recent Replies/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Replies');
		  }
		} 
		
		if ($('#search-tabbed').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Searched');
		}
		
		if ($('#group-tables').length &gt; 0) {
			DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Groups List');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/groups/) &amp;&amp; $("#group_info").length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Group', { name: $('.name').children('a').text() });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').click(function() {
		    var nickName;
		    nickName = ($(this).prev('a').html() || $('.name a').html())
		    if ($(this).hasClass('do-hotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Followed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    } else if ($(this).hasClass('do-unHotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Unfollowed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    }    
		  });
		}
	}
  </script>
